                    }
                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton ( Gossypium &#160; hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Biotechnology 2015 33:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2015-04-20"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2015 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Two draft sequences of Gossypium hirsutum, the most widely cultivated cotton species, provide insights into genome structure, genome rearrangement, gene evolution and cotton fiber biology. Gossypium hirsutum has proven difficult to sequence owing to its complex allotetraploid (AtDt) genome. Here we produce a draft genome using 181-fold paired-end sequences assisted by fivefold BAC-to-BAC sequences and a high-resolution genetic map. In our assembly 88.5% of the 2,173-Mb scaffolds, which cover 89.6%&#8764;96.7% of the AtDt genome, are anchored and oriented to 26 pseudochromosomes. Comparison of this G. hirsutum AtDt genome with the already sequenced diploid Gossypium arboreum (AA) and Gossypium raimondii (DD) genomes revealed conserved gene order. Repeated sequences account for 67.2% of the AtDt genome, and transposable elements (TEs) originating from Dt seem more active than from At. Reduction in the AtDt genome size occurred after allopolyploidization. The A or At genome may have undergone positive selection for fiber traits. Concerted evolution of different regulatory mechanisms for Cellulose synthase (CesA) and 1-Aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase1 and 3 (ACO1,3) may be important for enhanced fiber production in G. hirsutum."/>

itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Xun-Xu" data-author-popup="auth-Xun-Xu">Xun Xu</a></span><sup itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a>,<a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation"             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p><i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> has proven difficult to sequence owing to its complex allotetraploid (A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub>) genome. Here we produce a draft genome using 181-fold paired-end sequences assisted by fivefold BAC-to-BAC sequences and a high-resolution genetic map. In our assembly 88.5% of the 2,173-Mb scaffolds, which cover 89.6%<span class="stix">∼</span>96.7% of the A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub> genome, are anchored and oriented to 26 pseudochromosomes. Comparison of this <i>G. hirsutum</i> A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub> genome with the already sequenced diploid <i>Gossypium arboreum</i> (AA) and <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> (DD) genomes revealed conserved gene order. Repeated sequences account for 67.2% of the A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub> genome, and transposable elements (TEs) originating from D<sub>t</sub> seem more active than from A<sub>t</sub>. Reduction in the A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub> genome size occurred after allopolyploidization. The A or A<sub>t</sub> genome may have undergone positive selection for fiber traits. Concerted evolution of different regulatory mechanisms for Cellulose synthase (<i>CesA</i>) and 1-Aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase1 and 3 (<i>ACO1,3</i>) may be important for enhanced fiber production in <i>G. hirsutum</i>.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The allotetraploid species <i>G. hirsutum</i> L. is both the world's most important fiber crop and a model polyploid crop<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zhu, Y.-X. &amp; Li, F.G. The Gossypium raimondii genome, a huge leap forward in cotton genomics. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 55, 570–571 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d35856e971">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chen, Z.J. et al. Toward sequencing cotton (Gossypium) genomes. Plant Physiol. 145, 1303–1310 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d35856e974">2</a></sup>. The <i>Gossypium</i> genus originated from paleo-hexaploidy of a eudicot progenitor and subsequent diversification into eight diploid genome groups, including A–G and K<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wendel, J., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J.M. in Genetics and Genomics of Cotton, vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A.H.) 3–22 (Springer, New York, 2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d35856e981">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d35856e984">4</a></sup>. The A-genome diploids of the genus, which are native to Africa, diverged from the eudicot progenitor together with the Mexican-derived D-genome diploids about 5<span class="stix">∼</span>10 million years ago (MYA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e988">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35856e991">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wendel, J.F. &amp; Albert, V.A. Phylogenetics of the cotton genus (Gossypium): character-state weighted parsimony analysis of chloroplast-DNA restriction site data and its systematic and biogeographic implications. Syst. Bot. 17, 115–143 (1992)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d35856e994">7</a></sup>. These two species were reunited geographically around 1<span class="stix">∼</span>2 MYA by the transoceanic dispersal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wendel, J.F. New world tetraploid cottons contain old-world cytoplasm. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132–4136 (1989)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d35856e999">8</a></sup> of an A-genome ancestor resembling <i>G. arboreum</i> (AA) to the New World. Subsequently, the A-genome progenitor hybridized with a New World D-genome ancestor resembling <i>G. raimondii</i> (DD), followed by chromosome doubling. The resulting dicotyledonous allotetraploid cotton dispersed from the Americas to the western Pacific and diverged into at least five well-established allotetraploid <i>Gossypium</i> species (A<sub>t</sub>A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub>; 2<i>n</i> = 4<i>x</i> = 52, where 't' stands for tetraploid), including <i>G. hirsutum</i>, the Upland cotton that accounts for more than 90% of commercial cotton production worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wendel, J.F. New world tetraploid cottons contain old-world cytoplasm. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132–4136 (1989)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1030">8</a></sup>.</p><p>High-quality assembly of allopolyploid plant genomes is a formidable task because the genomes are large and have highly homeologous subgenomes. A draft genome of the 17-gigabase (Gb) monocotyledonous allohexaploid crop <i>Triticum aestivum</i> (2<i>n</i> = 6× = 42, AABBDD) was recently reported by an international consortium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1043">9</a></sup>. As a result of their concerted efforts, 61% of the scaffolds were anchored and oriented on 21 pseudochromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1047">9</a></sup>. In another study, 63% (712.3 Mb) of the 1,130-Mb allotetraploid (2<i>n</i> = 4× = 19, A<sub>n</sub>A<sub>n</sub>C<sub>n</sub>C<sub>n</sub>) <i>Brassica napus</i> genome was assembled. 18,278 of the <i>B. napu</i>s 20,702 scaffolds were assigned to either the A<sub>n</sub> or C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes, which constituted 19 chromosomes in total<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1074">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we sequenced and assembled the allotetraploid genome of <i>G. hirsutum</i> using DNA prepared from the highly homozygous TM-1 pure line<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kohel, R.J., Richmond, T.R. &amp; Lewis, C.F. Texas Marker-1. Description of a genetic standard for Gossypium hirsutum. Crop Sci. 10, 670–671 (1970)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1084">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Yu, J.Z. et al. A high-density simple sequence repeat and single nucleotide polymorphism genetic map of the tetraploid cotton genome. G3 (Bethesda) 2, 43–58 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1087">12</a></sup>. We compared the <i>G. hirsutum</i> assembly to the putative ancestral species, <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1096">6</a></sup> and <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1103">5</a></sup>, to investigate subgenome evolution and gene function including genes related to fiber biology.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Assembly of the <i>G. hir</i>s<i>utum</i> genome</h3><p>The allotetraploid genome of Upland cotton <i>G. hirsutum</i> has been estimated, using various methods, as 2.25–2.43 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E.D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208–218 (1991)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1129">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J.M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of Gossypium species. Ann. Bot. 95, 789–797 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1132">14</a></sup>. We generated a total of 445.7 Gb, or 181-fold haploid genome coverage, of raw paired-end Illumina reads by sequencing whole genome shotgun (WGS) libraries of homozygous cv. 'TM-1' with fragment lengths ranging from 250 bp to 40 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Owing to the existence of abundant repetitive sequences and homeologous chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 2</a>), we were unable to assemble this allotetraploid genome satisfactorily using only the WGS data. Supplemental use of a bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC-to-BAC) sequencing strategy substantially improved the assembly. A total of 100,187 BACs, that corresponded to about fivefold genome coverage, were sequenced and used in the final assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 1a,b</a>). Each BAC was assembled individually before genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 1c</a>). Genome assembly used sequenced BACs and paired-end data. A total of 2,173 Mb of the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome sequence was assembled using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1152">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1155">16</a></sup>, with the largest scaffold being 8.4 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Tab1">Table 1</a>). This corresponds to 96.7% of the previous estimation of nuclear DNA content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E.D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208–218 (1991)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1162">13</a></sup>, or 89.6% according to a more recent report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J.M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of Gossypium species. Ann. Bot. 95, 789–797 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1166">14</a></sup>. The N50 (the size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) of the contigs and scaffolds was 80 kb and 764 kb, respectively, which was better than the assembly that used WGS data only (N50 of contigs and scaffolds was 20 kb and 107 kb, respectively; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Tables 2 and 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.3208/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The quality of the assembly was assessed by aligning scaffolds to BAC sequences obtained using Sanger technology, and also by mapping available expressed sequence tags (ESTs) to the A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub> genome. All 223 sequenced BACs were recovered with &gt;96% sequence identity in our assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Of the 108,790 transcripts derived from transcriptome sequencing of <i>G. hirsutum</i>, 98.9% were detected in our assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Of the assembled genome, 1,923 Mb or 88.5% was anchored and oriented to 26 pseudochromosomes according to a high-resolution genetic map that we constructed based on 39,662 co-dominant single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers produced from the TM-1 × 3-79 recombinant inbred line (RIL) population of 167 individual lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Tables 6 and 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome duplications and tetraploidization</h3><p>All present-day allopolyploid cottons are thought to have arisen from a single common lineage derived from the hybridization of A<sub>2</sub> and D<sub>5</sub> genome ancestors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wendel, J., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J.M. in Genetics and Genomics of Cotton, vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A.H.) 3–22 (Springer, New York, 2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1551">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wendel, J.F. &amp; Cronn, R.C. in Advances in Agronomy (ed. Sparks, D.L.) 139–186 (Academic Press, 2003)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1554">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhang, H.B., Li, Y., Wang, B. &amp; Chee, P.W. Recent advances in cotton genomics. Int. J. Plant Genomics 2008, 742304 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1557">18</a></sup>. However, early polyploidization events in the evolution of the <i>Gossypium</i> genus occurred before the AADD lineage emerged. In comparisons of all sequenced <i>Gossypium</i> genomes with the seven ancestral chromosome groups reported in grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1571">19</a></sup>, we found that the paleopolyploidy was shared at orthologous positions from the ancestor to <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). These results confirm previous reports of the paleohexaploid origin of eudicot species such as <i>Theobroma cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1590">20</a></sup> and <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Myburg, A.A. et al. The genome of Eucalyptus grandis. Nature 510, 356–362 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1596">21</a></sup>. Clear-cut chromosomal DNA fragmentation patterns were observed in eudicot genomes that underwent zero (as in <i>V. vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1602">19</a></sup> and <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1608">20</a></sup>), one (as in all three cotton species) or two (as in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1615">22</a></sup> and <i>Glycine max</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1621">23</a></sup>) whole genome duplications during their evolution. It should be noted that chromosome 16 of <i>V. vinifera</i> might not have been assembled correctly, as it had much lower than expected percentages of remnant ancestral DNA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Evolution and syntenic analysis of the G. hirsutum genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Evolution and syntenic analysis of the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="968"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) <i>G. hirsutum</i> and six other genomes descended from common eudicot genome ancestors. Colored blocks within modern chromosomes of the species represent the chromatin origin from seven ancestral chromosomes. Numbers denote the predicted divergence times (MYA) and each red dot represents one whole genome duplication. (<b>b</b>) Syntenic blocks between the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome in <i>G. hirsutum</i> and the diploid A genome in <i>G. arboreum</i> genome. (<b>c</b>) Syntenic blocks between the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome in <i>G. hirsutum</i> and the diploid D genome in <i>G. raimondii</i>.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM18">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>According to our calculations, the ancient hexaploidization event shared among the eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tang, H. et al. Unravelling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1693">24</a></sup> and the more recent duplication event in the progenitor of <i>G. arboreum</i> (A genome) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (D genome) occurred around 115<span class="stix">∼</span>146 and 13<span class="stix">∼</span>20 MYA, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), which is similar to what was previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1706">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1709">6</a></sup>. An additional peak around 1.5 MYA that corresponds to the predicted hybridization and subsequent polyploidization event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wendel, J.F. &amp; Albert, V.A. Phylogenetics of the cotton genus (Gossypium): character-state weighted parsimony analysis of chloroplast-DNA restriction site data and its systematic and biogeographic implications. Syst. Bot. 17, 115–143 (1992)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1714">7</a></sup> was observed only in <i>G. hirsutum</i> (A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub> genome) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). We assigned the 26 pseudochromosomes of <i>G. hir</i>s<i>utum</i> to either the A<sub>t</sub> or D<sub>t</sub> subgenome based on syntenic relationships with its predicted diploid ancestors as well as the published genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Yu, J.Z. et al. A high-density simple sequence repeat and single nucleotide polymorphism genetic map of the tetraploid cotton genome. G3 (Bethesda) 2, 43–58 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1739">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig1">Fig. 1b,c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). The anchored A<sub>t</sub> subgenome (1,170 Mb) is larger than the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome (753 Mb), consistent with the sizes of the anchored A genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1754">5</a></sup> (1,530 Mb, 90.4% of 1,694 Mb) and D genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1758">6</a></sup> (567 Mb, 73.2% of 775 Mb). Co-linearity analysis of the current assembly indicated that the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome is of high quality (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Annotation and gene content</h3><p>We annotated 76,943 gene models in the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome by combining <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, homolog protein data search, EST alignment and assembly of RNA-seq reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 8</a>). In bread wheat, a total of 124,201 gene loci have been identified, and 101,040 have been identified in oilseed rape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1785">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1788">10</a></sup>. These and our data suggest that the coding capacities of a plant species are basically proportional to its polyploidism. About 84.5% of our gene models have homolog matches in databases, including KEGG and GO (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Of the gene models observed in <i>G. hir</i>s<i>utum</i>, 93.76% or 72,142 were evenly distributed along chromosomes, with 35,056 in the A or A<sub>t</sub> subgenomes and 37,086 in the D or D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes. Also, the Upland cotton genome encodes 602 microRNAs (miRNAs), 2,153 rRNAs, 2,050 tRNAs and 8,325 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><p>We scanned the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome, using an all-versus-all blastp approach, for syntenic blocks and found that the D<sub>t</sub> and A<sub>t</sub> subgenomes had high collinear relationships with the genomes of <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i>, respectively. There were 1,801 collinear blocks between <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i>, which covered 68.2% and 65.9% of the respective A genome or A<sub>t</sub> subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 11</a>). Similarly, we observed a total of 2,241 collinear blocks between <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i>, which covered 91.9% and 88.8% of the respective genome or subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative studies of cotton genomes</h3><p>Plant genomes have been massively invaded by transposable elements (TEs), many of which are located near host genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Oliver, K.R., McComb, J.A. &amp; Greene, W.K. Transposable elements: powerful contributors to angiosperm evolution and diversity genome. Genome Biol. Evol. 5, 1886–1901 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1864">25</a></sup>. The presence of TEs can activate or repress genes under specific biotic or abiotic conditions, or even at different developmental stages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Bennetzen, J.L. &amp; Wang, H. The contributions of transposable elements to the structure, function, and evolution of plant genomes. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 505–530 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1868">26</a></sup>. As much as 66% of the <i>G. hirsutum</i> assembly is composed of TEs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 12</a>). In common with <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1880">5</a></sup> and <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1887">6</a></sup>, the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome of <i>G. hirsutum</i> contains markedly higher amounts of <i>gypsy</i> than D<sub>t</sub>, whereas the reverse is true for <i>copia</i>. However, both <i>copia</i> and <i>gypsy</i> were substantially more actively transcribed in the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). The estimated insertion time of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, including both <i>copia</i> and <i>gypsy</i>, was determined using a spontaneous mutation rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1923">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). <i>Copia</i> elements were remarkably more active than <i>gypsy</i> (Student's <i>t</i>-test, <i>P</i> ≤ 0.05) in the recent 0<span class="stix">∼</span>1 MYA time frame (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), with higher proportions of <i>copia</i> located near coding genes than <i>gyp</i>s<i>y</i>-type (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>, Student's <i>t</i>-test, <i>P</i> ≤ 0.05). Our data indicate that the TEs of the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome tend to be more active than that of the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome after the tetraploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat Biotechnol. doi:
                    10.1038/nbt.3207
                    
                   (20 April 2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d35856e1969">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Characterization of copia and gypsy TEs in the G. hirsutum genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Characterization of <i>copia</i> and <i>gypsy</i> TEs in the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="180"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Statistics for these two types of TEs present in the D<sub>t</sub> (left half) and A<sub>t</sub> (right half) subgenomes. The outer circle shows the percent coverage of <i>copia</i> (green histogram) and <i>gypsy</i> (purple histogram) in nonoverlapping windows (window size = 500 kb). The following two inner circles indicate the <i>copia</i> and <i>gypsy</i> transcript levels, which were estimated by averaging values of reads (log<sub>10</sub>) from different tissues in nonoverlapping 500-kb windows. The links in the center indicate collinearity between A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes. Only syntenic blocks of &gt;1 Mb in length are shown. (<b>b</b>) Estimated insertion time for <i>copia</i> and <i>gypsy</i> LTR retrotransposons. (<b>c</b>) Distances from individual TEs to their nearest gene.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM19">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The different abundances of retrotransposons and DNA transposons (62.81% and 2.52%, respectively, in our paper versus 52.29% and 1.08%, respectively, in Zhang <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat Biotechnol. doi:
                    10.1038/nbt.3207
                    
                   (20 April 2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2054">28</a></sup>) was probably the result of using different methods to detect TEs. We used the <i>de novo</i> method and the homolog search method based on Repbase, whereas Zhang <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat Biotechnol. doi:
                    10.1038/nbt.3207
                    
                   (20 April 2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2064">28</a></sup> used the MIPS database. As a result, only 0.43% of the TEs found are recognized as unclassified elements in our analysis, whereas 11.38% unclassified elements are reported by Zhang <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat Biotechnol. doi:
                    10.1038/nbt.3207
                    
                   (20 April 2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2072">28</a></sup>. Using our methodology, a further 9.43% of their unclassified elements can be recognized as retrotransposons, and an additional 1.25% of the unclassified elements can be recognized as DNA transposons. The MIPS database is constructed strictly according to the published cotton genomes whereas Repbase contains all repeat sequences from various plant genomes, further accounting for differences in findings.</p><p>Gene order was constructed by quartet alignments among the four sister chromosomes from the three <i>Gossypium</i> species. Using an iterative method based on the best match and the syntenic gene finder in MCScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2082">29</a></sup>, we identified 32,466 and 32,811 orthologous gene pairs within the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes, respectively, in <i>G. hirsutum</i> and their corresponding ancestral A and D diploid genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM24">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Of the 28,592 orthologous gene pairs identified between <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>, 22,876 were also conserved homeologous gene pairs in <i>G. hirsutum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Of the orthologous gene pairs, 643 were lost from both <i>G. raimondii</i> and the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome of <i>G. hirsutum</i>, and 478 were lost from both <i>G. arboreum</i> and the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), which indicates that the ancestral D genome suffered a higher frequency of genic sequence losses. We further identified gene losses in syntenic blocks in which at least two of the four <i>Gossypium</i> genomes have orthologous genes: 523 and 461 genes were absent from A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub>, respectively, compared with 234 lost in the A genome and 390 lost in the D genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). These data suggest a higher rate of gene losses in the allotetraploid cotton than in both diploid species. Also, more genes were lost in the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome than the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome during the formation of <i>G. hirsutum</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolution of gene models, DNA fragments and syntenic blocks among G. hirsutum (AtDt) and two diploid cotton genomes, G. arboreum (A) and G. raimondii (D)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Evolution of gene models, DNA fragments and syntenic blocks among <i>G. hirsutum</i> (A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub>) and two diploid cotton genomes, <i>G. arboreum</i> (A) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (D).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="959"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Scenarios and statistics of gene conservation. Solid lines indicate currently observed genes, and dotted lines indicate lost genes. The numbers beneath each drawing represent the number of gene pairs found in the three different genomes that fit the specific model. From left to right, genes present in all four genomes, genes not observed in A<sub>t</sub>, genes not observed in D<sub>t</sub>, genes not observed in either A or A<sub>t</sub>, genes not observed in either D or D<sub>t</sub>, genes not observed in A, genes not observed in D. (<b>b</b>) HE of genomic segments between the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes in a region of <i>G. hirsutum</i> chromosome 9 (Gh9). The curves in the upper panel show homologous gene pairs between A<sub>t</sub> and A or A<sub>t</sub> and D. The lower panel shows the <i>K</i>s value distribution for syntenic blocks, which indicates HE in the tetraploid cotton. The dot plots show distribution of <i>K</i>s values and the boxplots display variations of <i>K</i>s values between A and D genomes. Note that some of the dots (outliers) are not included in the boxplots due to their low probabilities. (<b>c</b>) Distribution of <i>K</i>s values between four cotton genomes and <i>T. cacao</i> (upper panel) and single-nucleotide variation (SNV) rate (lower panel) among different cotton genomes.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM20">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Homeologous exchanges (HEs) among cotton genomes</h3><p>HEs, or transpositions, are characterized by the loss of a chromosomal region that is replaced by a duplicate copy of the corresponding homeologous region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2239">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Udall, J.A., Quijada, P.A. &amp; Osborn, T.C. Detection of chromosomal rearrangements derived from homeologous recombination in four mapping populations of Brassica napus L. Genetics 169, 967–979 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2242">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wright, R.J., Thaxton, P.M., El-Zik, K.M. &amp; Paterson, A.H. D-subgenome bias of Xcm resistance genes in tetraploid Gossypium (cotton) suggests that polyploid formation has created novel venues for evolution. Genetics 149, 1987–1996 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2245">31</a></sup>. The synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i>s values) of orthologous DNA are used to evaluate evolutionary distances among different plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yu, J. et al. The genomes of Oryza sativa: a history of duplications. PLoS Biol. 3, e38 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2252">32</a></sup>. In the A-genome-derived chromosome 9 of <i>G. hirsutum</i>, a syntenic block in the middle of the chromosome showed a very low <i>K</i>s value with a fragment from the D genome, whereas the two other blocks in this same region displayed low <i>K</i>s values to DNA fragments from the A genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), suggesting that this A<sub>t</sub> chromosome segment had undergone HE with a D<sub>t</sub> homeologous region. Potential HEs were supported by multiple paired-end reads to ensure that they did not result from misassemblies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Putative HEs with junction distances ≤7 kb were selected for PCR verification using primers designed from both sides of the particular junction, and five out of seven potential HEs were successfully amplified in <i>G. hirsutum</i>, but not in <i>G. arboreum</i> or <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Also, HE regions showed a considerably higher rate of multicopy genes than the genome as a whole, indicating that HEs may be associated with the evolution of high copy number genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Using this approach across all <i>G. hirsutum</i> chromosomes, we identified 100 possible HEs, of which 54 were D<sub>t</sub> to A<sub>t</sub> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 14</a>), 46 were A<sub>t</sub> to D<sub>t</sub> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Further analysis of syntenic blocks involved in HEs indicated that 1,790 genes were transferred from D<sub>t</sub> to A<sub>t</sub>, whereas 1,530 genes were transferred from A<sub>t</sub> to D<sub>t</sub>. The average <i>K</i>s values for collinearity-supported gene pairs were 0.463 for D<sub>t</sub> versus <i>T. cacao</i> and 0.494 for D versus <i>T. cacao</i>, with 0.422 for A<sub>t</sub> versus <i>T. cacao</i> and 0.476 for AA versus <i>T. cacao</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>, upper panel). The <i>K</i>s values of both A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes were substantially lower than those of the A and D diploid genomes, respectively. Similarly, analysis of intergenic collinear regions showed that the single-nucleotide variation rate for D<sub>t</sub> versus D was greater than that for A<sub>t</sub> versus A (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>, lower panel). These results indicate that the D or D<sub>t</sub> genome has higher mutation rates than A or A<sub>t</sub> genome, which is consistent with a previous report for the <i>Adh</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Small, R.L. &amp; Wendel, J.F. Differential evolutionary dynamics of duplicated paralogous Adh loci in allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium). Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 597–607 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2365">33</a></sup>. Further analysis revealed dN/dS ratios of 0.338 for D<sub>t</sub> versus <i>T. cacao,</i> 0.270 for D versus <i>T. cacao</i>, 0.404 for A<sub>t</sub> versus <i>T. cacao</i> and 0.314 for AA versus <i>T. cacao</i>. These data suggest that the A and A<sub>t</sub> genomes are undergoing less purifying selection and greater positive selection than the D and D<sub>t</sub> genomes; and that the genetic redundancy created by allotetraploidy may have allowed relaxed purifying selection in both the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Deletions and fiber development</h3><p>Ethylene is a key modulator of cotton fiber cell growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Shi, Y.H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fibre cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2403">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Qin, Y.M. et al. Saturated very-long-chain fatty acids promote cotton fibre and Arabidopsis cell elongation by activating ethylene biosynthesis. Plant Cell 19, 3692–3704 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2406">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Qin, Y.M. &amp; Zhu, Y.-X. How cotton fibres elongate: a tale of linear cell-growth mode. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 14, 106–111 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2409">36</a></sup>, and significant differences in ethylene or cell wall biosynthetic gene expression patterns during fiber development have been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Shi, Y.H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fibre cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2413">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Pang, C.Y. et al. Comparative proteomics indicates that biosynthesis of pectic precursors is important for cotton fibre and Arabidopsis root hair elongation. Mol. Cell. Proteomics 9, 2019–2033 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2416">37</a></sup>. Early experiments showed 50- to 500-fold increases in <i>ACO</i> transcript levels in <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> ovules, respectively, in comparisons with <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2432">5</a></sup>. When grown in a semi <i>in vivo</i> ovule culture system, <i>G. arboreum</i> ovules released almost undetectable levels of gaseous ethylene over a 15-d culture period, whereas <i>G. raimondii</i> produced large amounts of this molecule (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Although ovules of <i>G. hirsutum</i> did synthesize measurable levels of ethylene, the onset of this ethylene burst occurred at a much later stage with a lower peak value, as compared with <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Gel shift assays were performed to investigate possible molecular mechanisms for regulating the expression of <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> that are rate-limiting during ethylene biosynthesis in cotton ovules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Shi, Y.H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fibre cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2464">34</a></sup>. Sequence alignment showed the deletion of a 128-bp fragment that resulted in the loss of a single MYB binding site in <i>ACO1</i> promoters from both the A genome and the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>, upper panel). Significant mobility shift was observed only when fragment P6 from the D genome (either from D or D<sub>t</sub>) was incubated with nuclear extracts prepared from 10 d post-anthesis (DPA) <i>G. hirsutum</i> ovules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>, middle and lower panels). When compared with the GaACO3 promoter, deletion of a large DNA fragment (123-bp) was observed in the GrACO3 promoter. Further scrutiny revealed several short sequence insertions (indels of 8 to 16 bp) in GrACO3 promoter that created two new and additional MYB-binding sites in <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>, upper panel; and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). As a result, only fragment PVI from D<sub>t</sub> or D genome showed substantial mobility shift whereas the same PVI fragment from A<sub>t</sub> or A genome showed no obvious nuclear protein binding (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>, middle and lower panels). A much weaker and similar binding of nuclear extracts on the PII fragment was observed for all four types of <i>ACO3</i> promoters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>, middle and lower panels). When the D genome–derived P6 and PVI fragments from the <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> promoter regions were individually incubated with nuclear extracts prepared from <i>G. raimondii</i> ovules harvested at different developmental stages, we found that the nuclear protein binding activity reached its peak values around 3 DPA; these amounts were about four to five times the levels found in 10-DPA ovules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>, upper panel). In <i>G. hirsutum</i>, the highest binding activities to both <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> promoters appeared in 10-DPA ovules, but the peak intensities were less than 20% of those found in <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>, middle panel). As expected, no obvious DNA binding activity was found using nuclear extracts from <i>G. arboreum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>, lower panel).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Ethylene production and its regulatory mechanisms in three cotton species (G. raimondii, G. arboreum and G. hirsutum)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Ethylene production and its regulatory mechanisms in three cotton species (<i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i>).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="552"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Comparisons of ethylene production from cultured ovules collected at 1 DPA and cultured for 14 d, with air samples collected at the different time points as shown. Data reported are the mean ± s.e.m. from three independent ovule culture experiments, with triplicate measurements for each sample. (<b>b</b>,<b>c</b>) Electrophoretic mobility shift assays (EMSA) showing the specific binding complex on the P6 fragment of <i>ACO1</i> (<b>b</b>) and <i>ACO3</i> (<b>c</b>) promoters. <sup>32</sup>P-labeled probes were incubated with nuclear protein samples prepared from 10-DPA <i>G. hirsutum</i> ovules. Dotted lines shown on the top of each panel show the lost sequences in the corresponding genome with the red boxes representing MYB binding sites. In each panel, one representative EMSA obtained using probes originating from the D<sub>t</sub> or the A<sub>t</sub> subgenomes of <i>G. hirsutum</i> is shown in the middle, and data obtained using probes produced from <i>G. arboreum</i> (Ga) or <i>G. raimondii</i> (Gr) in the bottom. (<b>d</b>) Comparisons of the binding activity on P6 from <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> promoter regions of the three different cottons. Shown are data obtained from nucleoproteins prepared from 0-, 3-, 5-, 10-, 15- and 20-DPA ovules and incubated with P6 originating from <i>G. raimondii</i>, from the D<sub>t</sub> copy of <i>G. hirsutum</i> and from <i>G. arboreum</i>. Error bars, mean ± s.e.m. from three independent EMSA experiments. (<b>e</b>) Phylogenetic and evolutionary analysis of <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> promoter regions from <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>T. cacao</i>. Scale bars, 100 bp. Statistical significance was determined using one-way analysis of variance software. *<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, **<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, ***<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM21">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Phylogenetic analysis revealed that cotton <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> are in the same clade with <i>TcACO1</i> (Thecc1EG030320t1) and <i>TcACO3</i> (Thecc1EG002179t1) of <i>T. cacao</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>). <i>GrACO1</i> and <i>GrACO3</i> displayed 43.8% and 43.3% identity, respectively, in promoter regions with their counterparts in <i>T. cacao</i>. Several important MYB binding sites observed in either <i>TcACOs</i> or <i>GrACOs</i> were not found in <i>GaACOs</i>, suggesting that <i>G. raimondii</i> evolved from the common eudicot ancestor before <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wendel, J., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J.M. in Genetics and Genomics of Cotton, vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A.H.) 3–22 (Springer, New York, 2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2717">3</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9"><i>Ce</i>s<i>A</i> upregulation and fiber elongation</h3><p>Profound differences in fiber properties are found among the three cotton species. For example, <i>G. hirsutum</i> usually produces fibers &gt;3 cm in lengths, whereas <i>G. arboreum</i> produces fibers of 1.3<span class="stix">∼</span>1.5 cm long, and no spinnable fiber is produced by <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>, left panel). <i>G. hirsutum</i> fiber cells undergo fast elongation until <span class="stix">∼</span>30 DPA, whereas those of <i>G. arboreum</i> stop growth around 20 DPA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>, right panel). As a load-bearing polymer of plant cell walls, cellulose microfibrils, which are produced by <i>cellulose synthase A</i> (<i>CesA</i>), have a key role in the formation of the secondary cell wall for cell growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Peng, L.C., Kawagoe, Y., Hogan, P. &amp; Delmer, D. Sitosterol-β-glucoside as primer for cellulose synthesis in plants. Science 295, 147–150 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2763">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="McFarlane, H.E., Doring, A. &amp; Persson, S. The cell biology of cellulose synthesis. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 69–94 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2766">39</a></sup>. The <i>CesA</i> genes are divided among six major clades (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Figs. 9a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">10</a>) and genome-wide analysis indicates that <i>G. hirsutum</i> contains 32 <i>Ce</i>s<i>A</i> and 64 cellulose synthase-like (<i>CSL</i>) genes, representing a notable increase over the number of <i>CesA</i> and <i>CSL</i> genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2801">22</a></sup> and <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2807">20</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Figs. 9b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">11</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fiber growth, expression and potential regulatory mechanisms of genes important for cell wall biosynthesis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Fiber growth, expression and potential regulatory mechanisms of genes important for cell wall biosynthesis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3208/MediaObjects/41587_2015_Article_BFnbt3208_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="447"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Comparisons of Fiber lengths of the three cotton species (left), and growth rate analysis for <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i> (right). Error bars, mean ± s.d. (<b>b</b>–<b>d</b>) qRT-PCR analysis of primary and secondary cell wall biosynthesis genes using the A<sub>t</sub>- (indicated by blue lines) or A-originated (red lines) copy as the template. <i>UER</i>, UDP-4-keto-6-deoxy-<span class="u-small-caps">D</span>-glucose 3,5-epimerase 4-reductase; <i>UGP</i>, UDP-<span class="u-small-caps">D</span>-glucose pyrophosphorylase; <i>UGD</i>, UDP-<span class="u-small-caps">D</span>-glucose dehydrogenase. See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 19</a> for gene-specific primers. Error bars, mean ± s.d. (<b>e</b>) The evolution of regulatory mechanisms for fiber-specific and highly expressed genes from the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM22">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3208/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Primary cell wall components produced by UGD, UGP and UER are also important for cotton fiber growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Pang, C.Y. et al. Comparative proteomics indicates that biosynthesis of pectic precursors is important for cotton fibre and Arabidopsis root hair elongation. Mol. Cell. Proteomics 9, 2019–2033 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2892">37</a></sup>. Transcriptome analyses indicated that a significant number of the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome-originated genes were highly and specifically expressed during fiber development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 16</a>), and this was confirmed by QRT-PCR analyses over a longer growth period of time (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5b–d</a>). In all cases, genes that originated from the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome, but not their homeologs from the A subgenome, were expressed highly and specifically during either the primary (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5b,c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 17</a>) or the secondary (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a>) cell wall biosynthesis stages. Although a few <i>CSL</i> genes showed moderate fiber-specific upregulation, there was little difference between the A<sub>t</sub>- and A-derived copies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Table 18</a>). This fiber-specific and high-level expression of cell wall biosynthesis genes was coupled with the evolution of more <i>cis</i>-elements through point mutations, as evidenced on the A<sub>t</sub><i>CesA</i>8-1 promoter (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5e</a>, upper drawing). Transposition of a <i>Tgm1</i> (a subclass of TE) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5e</a>, middle drawing) and partial deletion of a MITE (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig5">Fig. 5e</a>, lower drawing) from the ancestral A genome seems to have contributed to the considerably increased number of pyrimidine boxes or the conserved MYC- and MYB-binding sites present on A<sub>t</sub><i>CesA</i>3-4 and A<sub>t</sub><i>UER2-2</i> promoters.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Deciphering the allotetraploid genome of <i>G. hirsutum</i> provides unique insights into the earliest stages of postpolyploidy evolution and the relationships between <i>G. hirsutum</i> and its ancestral diploid species. Both <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i> underwent cotton-specific whole genome duplication at <span class="stix">∼</span>16.6 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat.
