Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2974">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2977">6</a></sup> after sharing the paleohexaploidization event common to all eudicots that occurred <span class="stix">∼</span>130.8 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, F.G. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2982">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2985">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d35856e2988">22</a></sup>. We estimate that the modern allotetraploid <i>G. hirsutum</i> species resulted from hybridization of the two ancestral species about <span class="stix">∼</span>1.5 MYA, based on the calculations comparing A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes, with their respective A or D diploid ancestral species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3208#MOESM23">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Up to 100 DNA segments have been replaced in one subgenome by sequences from another subgenome. Although most coding genes were conserved among all four genomes, close scrutiny showed that a large number of these genes were transferred from D<sub>t</sub> to A<sub>t</sub>, so that the current A<sub>t</sub> subgenome may express more genes than the ancestral A genome. This segmental replacement apparently causes substantial differences in TE activities in the two subgenomes compared with the postulated ancestors and this seems to contribute to differences in fiber production and fiber quality of the allotetraploid species.</p><p>The assembly and analyses of the allotetraploid cotton genome show that the two subgenomes have different mutation rates because the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome has higher single-nucleotide variation and <i>K</i>s values than those of the A<sub>t</sub>. Also, the allotetraploid genome enjoys lower purifying selection pressure than the ancestral diploid genomes, because both D<sub>t</sub> and A<sub>t</sub> subgenomes have higher dN/dS ratios than the D and A diploid genomes. Finally, the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome may have undergone positive selection for fiber-related traits because the A<sub>t</sub> showed a higher dN/dS ratio than the D<sub>t</sub>. The significant changes in <i>K</i>s rates among HEs and their flanking sequences suggest that HEs may be associated with “gene conversion,” which involves homology-directed double-strand break repair between homeologous chromosomes or sister chromatids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Rozen, S. et al. Abundant gene conversion between arms of palindromes in human and ape Y chromosomes. Nature 423, 873–876 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3036">40</a></sup>. The <i>ACOs</i> and primary and secondary cell wall biosynthesis genes reported here might provide targets for engineering of improved fiber yield.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Materials.</h3><p>We used the highly homozygous Upland cotton (<i>G. hirsutum</i>) genetic standard line TM-1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kohel, R.J., Richmond, T.R. &amp; Lewis, C.F. Texas Marker-1. Description of a genetic standard for Gossypium hirsutum. Crop Sci. 10, 670–671 (1970)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3057">11</a>) for DNA sequencing. The homozygosity was reached through 51 successive generations of self-fertilization by single seed descent from one TM-1 seed. <i>G. hirsutum</i> cv. TM-1, <i>G. arboreum</i> cv. Shixiya1 and <i>G. raimondii</i> acc. D<sub>5</sub>-3 (CMD#10) plants were maintained in the greenhouse for extraction of DNA or RNA. The tetraploid <i>G. barbadense</i> cv. 3-79 was crossed with TM-1 to develop an RIL population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Yu, J.Z. et al. A high-density simple sequence repeat and single nucleotide polymorphism genetic map of the tetraploid cotton genome. G3 (Bethesda) 2, 43–58 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3076">12</a></sup>. A total of 167 RIL individuals were successfully sequenced for the construction of genetic maps.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">WGS sequencing.</h3><p>Genomic libraries were prepared following the manufacturer's standard instructions and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. To construct paired-end libraries, we fragmented DNA by nebulization with compressed nitrogen gas, and blunted DNA ends before adding an A base to each 3′-end. DNA adaptors with a single T-base 3′-end overhang were ligated to the above products. Ligation products were purified on 0.5%, 1% or 2% agarose gels, each of which targeted a specific range of insert sizes. We constructed <i>G. hir</i>s<i>utum</i> genome sequencing libraries with insert sizes of 250 bp, 350 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb, 20 kb and 40 kb. All libraries were sequenced on the Illumina sequencing platform, and paired-end reads from each library were obtained. To obtain high-quality reads for assembly, we first filtered out unusable reads, which included the following: (i) reads containing ≥10% “N” bases; (ii) reads with low-quality data (the ASCII value of base-64 ≤7) for 65% of the bases for short insert (&lt;2 kb) or 80% of the bases for long insert (≥2 kb), (iii) reads containing &gt;10-bp adaptor sequences, (iv) reads with &gt;10 bp that overlapped between two ends of reads of short inserts and (v) reads with identical sequences at the two ends.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">BAC pooling and sequencing.</h3><p>HindIII and BamHI were used to generate partially digested insert DNA for the construction of TM-1 BAC libraries. Insert sizes of these BAC libraries ranged between 80 kb and 180 kb on average. The Agilent Bravo Automated Liquid Handling Platform (Agilent) and Agilent BenchCel Microplate Handler (Agilent) were used for the construction of BAC libraries. The Adaptive Focused Acoustics (AFA) DNA fragmentation system (Covaris) was used to fractionate DNA samples of BACs. T4 DNA polymerase (Illumina) was used to convert overhangs resulting from fragmentation into blunt ends. Klenow fragment was used to add an A base to the 3′-end of each blunt DNA fragment for the ligation of index adapters having a single T base. DNA samples with different index adapters were pooled together, and DNA segments of 500 bp were selected for library construction. Subsequently, ten 96-well plates were pooled into a single lane for sequencing (i.e., 960 samples/lane), resulting in an average throughput of 100 million reads/lane. In total, 1,080 96-well samples, or 103,680 BAC clones, were obtained in which 100,187 BACs were sequenced successfully. For each BAC clone, a 500-bp paired-end library was constructed and sequenced with the HiSeq2000 sequencing platform. Each BAC clone was sequenced with &gt;100-fold coverage on average.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome assembly.</h3><p>The allotetraploid genome of <i>G. hirsutum</i> was assembled through a BAC-to-BAC strategy combined with WGS sequencing. The primary steps were as follows.</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">1</span>
                      
                        <p>We first assembled each BAC clone using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3123">16</a></sup> based on the BAC reads with expected read-length for each clone.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">2</span>
                      
                        <p>We also assembled all the WGS sequencing reads from the libraries with short insert sizes into contigs using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3138">16</a></sup>.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">3</span>
                      
                        <p>All BAC assemblies and all the contigs assembled from the WGS sequencing were pooled into the overlap-layout-consensus (OLC) assembly, identical sequences were merged and redundant bases were filtered out from overlapping lengths using software contained within the Rabit package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="You, M. et al. A heterozygous moth genome provides insights into herbivory and detoxification. Nat. Genet. 45, 220–225 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3153">41</a></sup>. Then, we obtained the nonredundant super-contig sequences.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">4</span>
                      
                        <p>All the super-contigs obtained from the OLC assembly were linked to scaffolds using all the WGS reads from long inserts (2–40 kb) in a step-by-step fashion using SSPAC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C.V., Jansen, H.J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3168">42</a></sup> with the following command: perl SSPACE_v1-1.pl -l lib.txt -s final3200.fa -k 3 -n 20 -x 0.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">5</span>
                      
                        <p>To fill the gaps (N regions) within the scaffold, all the WGS reads from libraries with short insert sizes were mapped to the scaffold, and the reads located in the gap regions were then locally assembled with the following GapCloser command: GapCloser -a scafSeq -b gap_all.lib -o scafSeq.FG1 -t 24.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                  </ol><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genetic map construction.</h3><p>Genetic linkage maps were constructed for anchoring the scaffolds to 26 chromosomes of the A<sub>t</sub>D<sub>t</sub> genome, using 167 individual RIL lines derived from an interspecifc cross of the <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> genetic standards TM-1 and 3-79 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Yu, J.Z. et al. A high-density simple sequence repeat and single nucleotide polymorphism genetic map of the tetraploid cotton genome. G3 (Bethesda) 2, 43–58 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3204">12</a>). Genomic DNA from this mapping population was digested with the restriction endonuclease EcoRI and processed into restriction-site-associated DNA or 'RAD' libraries. Every 24 cotton individuals were pooled into one sequencing library with nucleotide multiplex identifiers (4, 5, 6, 7 and 8 bp), and each sequencing library was barcoded. Approximately 3,940 Mb of 90-bp reads (23 Mb of read data for each progeny on average) was generated on the HiSeq 2000 next-generation sequencing platform. The SNP calling process was carried out using the GATK pipeline. JoinMap4.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Stam, P. Construction of integrated genetic linkage maps by means of a new computer package: JoinMap. Plant J. 3, 739–744 (1993)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3208">43</a>) was used to conduct the linkage analysis. We used the method described in binmap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Xie, W. et al. Parent-independent genotyping for constructing an ultrahigh-density linkage map based on population sequencing. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 10578–10583 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3212">44</a></sup> to combine the adjacent SNP markers into bins that can integrate markers with high quality and filter the mistakes among them. The primary steps were as follows: (i) using MPR (Maximum Parsimony of Recombination) to infer a parental genotype; (ii) using Bayesian inference to acquire reliable parents' genotype; (iii) using HMM (Hidden Markov Model) to correct the progeny genotype. We finally obtained 6,501 bins and utilized them in anchoring the scaffolds together with other SNP markers.</p><p>A logarithm of odds (LOD) score of 10 was initially set as the linkage threshold for linkage group identification. All high-quality markers were used to construct the consensus maps with the RIL population in JoinMap4.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Stam, P. Construction of integrated genetic linkage maps by means of a new computer package: JoinMap. Plant J. 3, 739–744 (1993)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3218">43</a>). We calculated the recombination fractions between all pairs of SNP markers in a scaffold and chose the SNP marker that had the minimum recombination fraction in the sum. A scaffold order was determined by tag SNPs, and inner SNPs were then used to orient the scaffolds. A complete set of 26 pseudochromosomes of <i>G. hirsutum</i> was produced, with chromosomes 1–13 corresponding to the A<sub>t</sub> subgenome and chromosomes 14–26 to the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Repeat content.</h3><p>We detected the repeat content of the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome using two methods, the <i>de novo</i> method and the homolog search method based on Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jurka, J. Repbase Update: a database and an electronic journal of repetitive elements. Trends Genet. 16, 418–420 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3243">45</a></sup>. Three <i>de novo</i> software packages, PILER-DF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Edgar, R.C. &amp; Myers, E.W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i152–i158 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3250">46</a></sup>, RepeatModeler and LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3255">47</a></sup>, were used to predict TE sequences in the genome. Intact LTRs were predicted using LTR_FINDER, and the LTR insert time was calculated according to the rate of spontaneous mutations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3259">27</a></sup>. The homology-based approach involved applying commonly used databases of known repetitive sequences. We identified TEs at the DNA level with RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3263">48</a></sup> using Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jurka, J. Repbase Update: a database and an electronic journal of repetitive elements. Trends Genet. 16, 418–420 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3267">45</a></sup> TE. We identified TEs at the protein level with RepeatProteinMask, which runs WuBlastX against the TE protein database. Then we combined the results from the DNA level and protein level and filtered out TE elements for which &gt;80% of their domains were contained within the domain of a higher-scoring match. We then joined the overlapping TE elements that match the same TE in the library.</p><p>Tandem repeats in the genome assembly were identified using Tandem Repeat Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3274">49</a></sup> with the following parameters: 2 7 7 80 10 50 2000 -d -h. We also searched noninterspersed repeats in the genome using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3278">48</a></sup> with its “-noint” parameter.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene prediction.</h3><p>We used <i>de novo</i> gene prediction, homology-based methods and RNA-seq data to predict gene structure and integrated all the results by GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3293">50</a></sup> to obtain a consensus gene set.</p><p>1.<i>De novo</i> gene prediction.</p><p>Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3306">51</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Majoros, W., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: Twoopen source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3310">52</a></sup> were used to obtain <i>de novo</i>–predicted gene structures from a version of the genome in which repeats were masked based on identified repeat sequences with lengths &gt;500 bp, except for miniature inverted–repeat transposable elements (MITEs), which are usually found near genes or inside introns.</p><p>2.Homology-based prediction.</p><p>Protein sequences of five sequenced genomes (<i>A. thaliana, G. raimondii, G. arboreum, C. papaya and T. cacao</i>) were aligned to the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome using TBLASTN (E-value ≤ 1E-5). Then the homologous genome sequences were aligned against the matching proteins using Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3330">53</a></sup> to generate accurate spliced alignments. The expressed sequence tag sequences of <i>G. hirsutum</i> were aligned against the genome sequence using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT: The BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3337">54</a></sup> (identity ≥ 0.95 and coverage ≥ 0.90) to generate spliced alignments. Then, Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3342">53</a></sup> was applied to exploit the spliced alignments of expressed sequences to automatically model gene structure.</p><p>3.RNA-seq–based prediction.</p><p>To assist in gene annotation, we first mapped RNA-seq reads of six transcriptomes from cotton leaf and fiber to the assembled genome using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3352">55</a></sup> to identify potential exons in addition to donor or receptor sites with the following parameters: –p 4-max-intron-length 20,000 –m 1 –r 20-mate-std-dev 20. Then we combined TopHat mapping results and applied Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3356">55</a></sup> to predict transcript structures with the following parameters: –I 20,000 –p. To obtain complete gene models, we used the fifth-order Markov model to predict ORFs.</p><p>4.Integration of evidence for gene prediction.</p><p>The GLEAN software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3366">50</a></sup> was used to integrate data derived from the three methods into a GLEAN-derived gene set. Short genes with coding sequences &lt;150 bp and low-quality genes (gaps in &gt;10% of the coding region) were filtered out.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Syntenic and <i>K</i>s analysis.</h3><p>All-versus-all BLASTP (e-value &lt; 1e-5) was used to detect orthologous and paralogous genes among <i>G. hirsutum, G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>. Syntenic blocks (with at least five genes per block) were identified by MCScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3388">29</a></sup> (MATCH_SCORE: 50, MATCH_SIZE: 5, GAP_SCORE: –3, E_VALUE: 1E-05). For the alignment results between these, each aligned block represented the orthologous pair derived from the common ancestor, and the sequences that contained the genes were used to show the intergenome relationships with their length information. Syntenic blocks were used to find homeologous exchanges (HEs) between the two subgenomes of tetraploid cotton by tracing the presence of A genome–derived haplotype blocks in the D<sub>t</sub> genome, and D-derived blocks in the A<sub>t</sub> genome. <i>K</i>s values along the chromosome between tetraploid and two diploid genomes were calculated in nonoverlapping windows (window size = five genes). HE was defined when one fragment with a few sequent windows from A<sub>t</sub> chromosome showed a lower <i>K</i>s value (Student's <i>t</i>-test, <i>P</i> ≤ 0.05) with a fragment from D genome than from A genome, whereas both flanking fragments displayed low <i>K</i>s values with fragments from the A genome, and vice versa. <i>K</i>s (the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site) and dN/dS ratios (the ratio of the number of nonsynonymous substitutions per nonsynonymous site to the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site) were calculated using yn00 in the PAML software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3418">56</a></sup> package with the branch model. To distinguish HEs and other DNA blocks with different mutation rates, the distribution of <i>K</i>s values for the whole chromosome was compared with that of the particular HE. Only the DNA blocks with <i>K</i>s values significantly deviating from the distribution of global <i>K</i>s values (Student's <i>t</i>-test, <i>P</i> ≤ 0.05) from both A and D genomes were acknowledged as candidate HEs. To avoid the recognition of possible misassembly as HEs, all the candidate HE regions with 50-kb flanking sequences were analyzed using the paired-end relation of reads from the libraries with insert sizes of 500 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb, 20 kb and 40 kb.</p><p>The 4DTv value of the blocks was calculated using a revised version of the HKY model. Gene family is defined here as a group of genes that are determined to descend by OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Remm, M., Storm, C.E. &amp; Sonnhammer, E.L. Automatic clustering of orthologs and in-paralogs from pairwise species comparisons. J. Mol. Biol. 314, 1041–1052 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3441">57</a></sup> from the last common ancestor of the species under consideration. First, BLASTP was used to compare all the protein sequences with a database containing a protein data set of all species with e-value &lt; 1e-5. Then clustering of genes was carried out with OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Remm, M., Storm, C.E. &amp; Sonnhammer, E.L. Automatic clustering of orthologs and in-paralogs from pairwise species comparisons. J. Mol. Biol. 314, 1041–1052 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3445">57</a></sup> (inflation parameter: 1.5).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analysis of ACOs and cell wall biosynthesis genes.</h3><p><i>ACO</i>, <i>CesA</i>, <i>UER</i>, <i>UGP</i> and <i>UGD</i> gene families were identified using the HMMER 3.0 software package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Finn, R.D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S.R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W29–W37 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3471">58</a></sup>. MEGA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731–2739 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3208#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d35856e3476">59</a></sup> was used to construct the phylogenetic tree with the neighbor-joining method. Double-stranded DNA sequences corresponding to different fragments of the <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> promoters, respectively, were synthesized by Invitrogen and labeled with <sup>32</sup>P to generate the DNA probes. DNA-protein interactions were performed in 20-μl reactions that contained 1.5 μl 10× binding buffer (12.5 mM HEPES, 50% glycerol, 200 mM KCl, 2.5 mM EDTA, 5 mM DTT), 1 μg poly dI,dC (Sigma-Aldrich), 20 μM labeled DNA probe and 20 μg nuclear extract and were then incubated at room temperature for 1 h. The reactions were analyzed with 8% native PAGE in 0.5× Tris-borate-EDTA at 120 V. DNA binding activity was quantified using the Typhoon 9200 PhosphorImager (GE Healthcare).</p><p>To analyze ethylene gas production, 96 freshly collected 1-DPA wild-type ovules from each cotton species were cultured in 96-well plates in the dark at 30 °C for 14 d. Air samples (50 μl) were collected at each time point and injected into a 30-m HP-PLOT column (J&amp;W) and were analyzed with a gas chromatograph (GC-14C; Shimadzu) equipped with a ñame-ionization detector. Ethylene production was determined by comparing with a standard ethylene sample run on the same GC using identical program. QRT-PCR experiments were performed as previously reported.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Accession codes.</h3><p>The <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome sequence, including all contigs and scaffolds, has been deposited at NCBI BioProject under the accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA259930">PRJNA259930</a> and also available at <a href="http://cgp.genomics.org.cn">http://cgp.genomics.org.cn</a>. Sequence data for <i>G. hirsutum</i> transcriptome analyses are available in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA180756">SRA180756</a>.</p></div></div></section>

Genet.</i> <b>46</b>, 567–572 (2014).</p><p class="c-article-references__links u-hide-print"><a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="/articles/cas-redirect/1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXotFeru7g%253D" aria-label="View reference 5 on CAS">CAS</a> 
    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fng.2987" aria-label="View reference 5">Article</a> 
    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 5 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Genome%20sequence%20of%20the%20cultivated%20cotton%20Gossypium%20arboreum&amp;journal=Nat.%20Genet.&amp;volume=46&amp;pages=567-572&amp;publication_year=2014&amp;author=Li%2CFG">

R.J.K., R.G.P. and J.Z.Y conceived the project, provided the homozygous seeds and revised the manuscript. Y.Z., C.L., C.Z., G.X. and H.S. wrote the manuscript.
